Here begins the twenty-fourth chapter.
Of the cure of flesh stuck in the nose.
When in the nose are grown various flesh tumors of which there is some cause, which is similar to the scorpion sting or to a polyp stone; and this malady is called polypus because it represents that stone in appearance. And from it is cancerous flesh, scaly, of a dusky colour; and from it is that which is not dusky in colour but leonine flesh.
Therefore, when you are to treat one who has this flesh but not fraudulent or cancerous, let him fast between his fingers and be laid in the sun, and open up his nose and stick your thumb into that flesh. Then extract it to the edges outwardly, and then cut off what you have grasped of that flesh with a sharp, pointed instrument, until you know that all the flesh has been removed; for if anything is left in there from which removal is possible, then you should treat it with one of the subtle instruments with ease so that nothing is left. But if it is bleeding or has an acidic pustule, then you should stop that with what the patient holds; or if it is one of the malignant pustules, then you should cut and cauterize that until blood is let out and all the cavities are opened.
Then let it be treated in the nose near where the incision was made with wine or water or vinegar. But if the nose is open and from it flows moisture into the palate, then know that it has healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you should know that within it is flesh grown in the pustules over bones of the nose to which no removal has come about.
Therefore, then let the patient swallow bile from which there is some thickness, and suck in his mouth seeds of nuts, and place between each nut the quantity of a finger or less, and cause the patient to yawn and insert one end of the bile into the nose with the ray or another cause by which it is possible.
And after this has been done, let him blow his breath into it until it reaches the passages of the nose, and spit out onto his handkerchief, for troops of children do this action in schools, and it is a lighter cause for him who wishes to do so.
Then place the two ends of the bile, one end that comes out over the nose and the other that goes into the nose, and then administer cutting of flesh with the nuts that are in the bile, and do this until you know that the flesh has been cut by the nuts in the bile.
Then extract the bile and place in the nose, near where the blood is being let out, a sponge, and do this for three days or more until the gum has corroded all that which is left of the flesh.
And at the end of the treatment, let a lead tube be placed in the nose for several days until it is healed.
But if there are other remedies that dry out, use that one.
And this is the form of the instrument to the purging of the nose through which oils and medicines are distilled into the nose:
And make it from silver or iron, which is similar to a small open lamp, like a small cauldron, and the mead similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed tube like a cane, and the canal of the instrument to the purging of the nose be round, uncovered; to which is attached in its back, with which you grasp it, when you heat in it oil or water that you wish from the sucks and the causative remedies.
Here begins the twenty-fifth chapter.
Of warts on the ends of the nose.
Warts are often found on the ends of the nose, and it grows large and swells in several days unless proper care is taken of it.
And for this reason you should dig into the beginning of its appearance, and cut off all of that. Then place on top of it or cauterize or burn with a medicine that is burning in that location.
But if its growth is delayed so that it becomes large, then consider if it has become hard, of dusky colour and of little feeling, then do not touch it with iron which is an evil apoplexy; for many times when you cut into this apoplexy, it causes great harm to the one who has it.
And if the apoplexy is of leonine touch and not of dusky colour, and its incision is possible in all of that, then evacuate the patient and cut into it without fear, and treat the location with what flows out and constrict it until it is healed.
Here begins the twenty-sixth chapter.
Of sewing up of the nose and of the lips and of the ear when the continuity is dissolved by pus or similar.
Know that when dissolution of continuity happens in one of these cartilages, few give way to that operation except for some men.
Therefore, when because of some cause to these, you should consider if it is fresh pus with blood, then you should bring together the two lips of the pus with sewing, and then treat that until it is healed.
And if the lips of dissolution of continuity are already separated, and each one is healed, then you should cut off each part of the visible vein until blood flows out. Then bring together the two lips with sewing and strengthen those, and over that powder blood of dragon’s blood and pound it, and place over the powder a poultice of diaphoretic plaster, or of one of the other plasters that are consolidative, and let it lie for two days or three, and then remove it and change the plaster and let it lie until the veins are cut off, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
But the manner of sewing is that you add the dissolution of continuity or with nails as we have recorded in sewing up of the belly, or add the dissolution of continuity with bile as we will tell later.
Here begins the twenty-seventh chapter.
Of extraction of those causes that come in both lips in the openings between teeth.
Many times in some men are found in the openings between teeth of their lips some hard pustules that resemble warts, and some are small and some are large.
Therefore you should open up the lips and split each one of those hard spots all around, and then fill up that space with sagion pounded until the blood is constricted, and then wash it with wine water and treat the location with what is constricted until it is healed.
Here begins the twenty-eighth chapter.
Of cutting of flesh joined to the gums.
Many times grow on the gum flesh that is joined which the ancients called albus, so then you should suspend it with wax or what you have that one with tongs and cut off that with your knife until its root, and let the pus flow out or the blood.
Then spread on the wound a mixture of powdered styptic or astringent herbs, or both.
And if the tissue comes back, cut it again and cauterize it; for then the cautery will not return.
Chapter 29.
Of toothache.
When in the surface of the teeth of the gums there are sharp, rough spots and holes, and between the gums there is corruption and the teeth become loose.
So you should open the patient's mouth and place his head in your lap, and hold his teeth so that they do not move, and examine the teeth where there are holes, and remove the cause of them; and this will cure the blackness and citrinity and other things which remain inside.
If this has happened in the beginning of the disease, then it is good; but if not, you must repeat the treatment on those for a second or third day until it reaches its conclusion as you desire.
Know that teeth have different needs of many remedies of diverse forms and figures according to how it is prepared for your operation. And the scaler with which teeth are scaled from their roots is different from the scaler with which they are scraped between them according to other forms; and you should prepare these forms of scrapers as soon as you see them:
Chapter 30.
Of tooth extraction.
You should cure the tooth of its pain with every artifice and delay in any extraction, for there is no cause to suspect that it will grow back when it has been extracted, for it is a sublime substance which causes suspicion in the extraction of it.
But if the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be certain which is the aching tooth, for often the pain deceives the patient and may be in a healthy tooth, and you extract that and the pain does not cease until the aching and sickly tooth has been extracted, for we have seen this through the operation of the barber.
So how you should be certain which is the aching tooth and if you know it well, then you must scarify the gum around the tooth with a spatula on which there is some virtue, so that it may be dissolved from the gum entirely, and then pull at it gently with your fingers or with subtle forceps little by little until you loosen it.
And then hold him strongly by the jaw with great persuasion or stupor until he cannot move, and place his head between your knees so that you may not harm him, and pull the tooth according to truth without breaking it nor giving the patient more pain.
And if it is not loose, then take one of those instruments and place it beneath the tooth from all sides with ease and pull at its removal so that it is done in the beginning.
But if the tooth is split or worn away, then you must fill that crack with a thread and squeeze it with the tip of your finger so that it does not get caught when you tighten it in your grip.
And you must open widely with scarification around the gum entirely and be careful of your strength so that you do not break it or tear it; then return to the patient for the second pain is greater than the first.
And beware of doing that which ignorant barbers do in their skill and audacity about extraction, without using what we have recommended; for they often bring great sickness on people, which are difficult to be tied up, and the roots of them drawn out entirely, or some of those, or that you extract it with some bone of the jaw so as we have often witnessed.
And then after its extraction, wash the mouth with wine and vinegar or wine and salt.
And if afterwards blood flows from it, as often, then take some alum and sprinkle it over the wound; and if that alum does not stop it, cauterize it.
The form of the forceps and subtile tongs with which it is gripped at the beginning should be of long length, short handle and thick, so that they do not bend when you must work with them.
And let them be of such gross handles so that when you tighten them, they do not bend; let their tips be short.
And let them be made of Indian iron formed from straight pieces; and at the tips of those should be teeth which one intrudes into the other and tighten with a strong grip; and sometimes the tips of those are in the form of a hawk's beak, so that it is hooked, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter 31.
Of tooth extraction and the cutting out of bones from the jaws that are broken.
When you must extract a tooth which is broken, then you should put over the wound some cotton for one or two days until it softens; and then insert your fingers or forceps the tips of which are fashioned like a hawk' s beak or hooked, the form of the forceps:
The tips of them are made from inside as with a file or chisel.
And if it does not obey your command with these forceps or tongs, then you should cut away around the root and strip off all the flesh and insert the instrument called an awl, which is a little lever, the form of which is this:
And it should have a short thick tip, and not bent so that it does not get caught.
So if by that the root is loose, it is good; but if not, help yourself with these other instruments of which these are their forms: the form of the first instrument is triangular at its tip in which there is some thickness:
The form of the second instrument is that it be triangular and more slender at its tip.
And you are helped with this instrument having two ramps, the form of which is thus: And with other instruments used in grinding teeth; for youth is done with this instrument which is fashioned like the testicle of a ram, and this is its form:
Of the triangular tip bent or short, in which there is some thickness, and not bent so that it does not get caught.
And know that the instruments of teeth are most like other instruments.
But the wise and subtle artisans of this art find out for themselves according to what indicates the operation and various sicknesses; for there are sicknesses to which the ancients did not assign nor prepare a proper instrument, due to the forms of their diverse species.
But if the jaw or other bones are broken, or if it is swollen, then let it be bound up on its place with that which is convenient to it of the instruments and forceps we have spoken of in the extraction of the tooth roots; and help yourself with a bandage the form of which is this:
And let it be bound up little by little with something thick, and let it be bound or held at the mouth so that it may not escape or open until all of its bone is loose; then restore the wound with convenient medicines to that.
But if there is some inflammation in the bone, then scrape it of that inflammation and its blackness, and cure it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of teeth protruding over others.
When teeth protrude beyond their natural size, particularly in women and especially in the elderly, one must consider: if a tooth stands alone and is not attached to another tooth, and there is no way to apply pressure or a poultice to it, then extract that tooth; but if it is attached to another tooth, then one should cut into it with this instrument, which has a shape like the mouth of a pig and is made of Indian iron with an extremely sharp edge.
And your incision should be the size through which you will cut into it over several days, according to the hardness of the tooth, and so that none of the other teeth are affected.
And if a protruding tooth is exposed to air, then file it down with an Indian iron file of this shape:
And it should all be made of Indian iron with an edge that is evenly sharp; and it should have tiny points like the file with which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed a little at a time over several days, so that no other tooth is affected or damaged. Then smooth it out at the end and polish it with some razor blades.
And if a tooth that is bitten into and pressed down upon by the tongue, then one should smooth out that spot where it is bitten so that the sharpness of that portion is blunted and flattened, so that the tongue is not cut nor speech impaired.
Chapter 33.
Of the restoration of damaged teeth with gold or silver.
When teeth suffer concussion and displacement from a blow or from falling down, and the patient cannot bear to chew on that side, and your treatment with astringent medicines has not worked, then so long as they are not loose there is skill in strengthening them with gold rather than silver; for silver turns green and soon wears away after a few days, but gold never changes color.
And the gold should be of such fineness and thickness according to how much can fit between the teeth; and the technique of the filling is that you take the gold and make twice as much as you need between two healthy teeth, and then let it grow with two curves of gold between two damaged teeth, or if only one or several are damaged, so that it reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the process at the beginning of the damaged portion that you started with, and strengthen your hand with ease and skill so that it is not damaged.
And your compression as you tighten the gold should not let go of the roots of the teeth so that it does not slip out.
Then cut off the excess gold with forceps or tweezers, and press it into each spot with your fingers, and shape and smooth it out between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth so that it does not cut the tongue. Then let it harden as tight as a Roman road.
But if it is loose or broken, strengthen it with another filling in the same way at all times.
And this is the form of teeth and the shape of the filling between two healthy teeth and two damaged ones, as they are bound together thus:
And when one or two teeth return to their sockets after falling out and are regrown as we have described, and become loose again:
And it is not easy except for skilled and wise artisans.
And sometimes a cow’s tooth is prepared for some reason, and it is made into the shape of a tooth, and placed in the place where the tooth fell out, and strengthened as we have said, and it remains and serves for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Of the incision of the membrane that appears on the tongue, and prohibits speech.
This membrane which sometimes appears underneath the tongue, either naturally or accidentally due to an illness that has already healed.
And the operation on it is that you open the patient’s mouth, lower their jaw; and raise their tongue, and then cut into that nerve membrane according to how far it is relaxed from the incision.
But if there is any hardness or swelling in it, and this is caused by the healing of an illness, then you should put some oil on it and cut into it where you first cut into the membrane and where the swelling has dissolved.
And be careful that there is no fissure in the flesh of the tongue, and that you cut through the artery and cause bleeding.
Then wash the cut with vinegar or warm water, and then place the tongue back into its socket; for it is a membrane that the patient’s tongue is kept in on each night so that it does not heal over again.
But if bleeding occurs, then put powdered vitriol on it; but if the blood clots, then cauterize the spot with a red-hot cautery that is suitable for such a thing. Then heal it according to the other causes by which it was caused.
Chapter 35.
Of the extraction of a ranula from under the tongue.
Sometimes an ulcer appears underneath the tongue that is similar to a small nut, which prevents the tongue from performing its natural function; and it is enlarged as it fills the mouth.
And the operation on it is that you open the patient’s mouth, looking at the ulcer in front of their face; and you consider the ulcer:
And if you see it as black or dark colored, hard, to which the patient does not respond with feeling, then do not touch it because it is rotten.
And if it is white and soft, then put oil on it, and cut into it with fine spatulae; and if blood comes out in the hour of your operation, then press it gently with a sponge, as if it were vitriol, so that the bleeding stops.
Then return to your work until you have completed it, after which wash it with warm water and vinegar, and heal it according to what caused it so that it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Of the extraction of an ulcer from two ears, and of that which is found in the gullet, and of other ulcers.
When pus gathers in the gullet and becomes hard like a gland, the pus of which comes out and is called two ears, when you heal it with what we have said in the previous chapter and it is not healed, then consider whether the ulcer is hard, dark colored, without feeling, then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is red colored and the vein is thick, then do not cauterize it with iron because of the flow of blood; before you let it heal itself, or else it will burst on its own.
And if it is white colored, raw, and the vein is fine, then that is what should be cut into.
And the operation on it is that you consider, before your operation, if the ulcer has rested completely after being cauterized or if it has diminished at all from any diminishment; and then let the patient look towards the sky, and his jaw should be lowered so that his chin is on his chest, and open his mouth with the help of a noble instrument, of which this is the shape:
And it should be made of silver or of steel, and it should be fine like a needle.
Therefore, when you press it with your finger and the pustule is shown to you, cut it with your knife, then grab it with tweezers and place it in the poultice, and pull it out to the outside as much as you can. Another thing is that you remove some cause from the tissues, and then cut it with an instrument of which this is the shape:
Another thing is that you remove some cause from the tissues, and then cut it with an instrument of which this is the shape: and is like a pair of forceps, except that its ends are curved so that the hole in each of them faces in the opposite direction, and they are very sharp; it should be made out of iron and plated with tin.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then cut it with a spatula whose shape is as follows: and it should be sharp on one side and blunt on the other side.
And after you have cut one wound, cut the other according to this method of cutting, and then wash the patient with water rubbed with salt.
But if blood flows from it, treat it with water in which the bark of a pomegranate or bay leaf has been boiled; and also use remedies that constrict the flow of blood, until it is healed.
And sometimes pus will appear in the armpit, and you should cut it according to what has already been said about cutting two wounds at the same time.
And I once treated a woman who had an abscess that had formed in her armpit; it was a dark color and didn't hurt much, and it had closed off her armpit; the woman could barely breathe, she had to be fed and given water to drink, and she would have died if this had gone on for one or two more days.
And the abscess was so large that it had pushed out for almost two months through two openings.
So I cut it open and placed one of their tweezers in it, and pulled out a great deal from her; then I cut it where I understood the other opening to be, and also closed off its mouth, pressed her tongue, and placed the tweezers inside the abscess.
And I cut out a large portion of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Then the armpit of that woman was completely closed off, and she began drinking water right away and also eating food; so I did not stop cutting that abscess many times over a long period, and it kept forming in the same place as before.
Then I gauged and cauterized her armpit, then bound it with an additional bandage; and I walked around and did not know what God had done to her.
Chapter 37.
On cutting of a hovel when it is pustular.
When pus drains from the nose and it is pustular and long, then it is called a perpendicular; and if it is thick and round at its lower end, then it is called a bulla.
But if it is cured by what has already been said in the division and does not agree with your treatment, and it sometimes happens that pus is drawn out from it and it is thin; and if it is round at its end and blood-red or black in color and not painful to the patient, then beware of cutting it, for there is danger in cutting it.
And when you see it according to the way that I have said about its whiteness and length, then press the patient's tongue with an instrument whose shape you know; and then place one of the tweezers in the pustule, pull it down and cut it with either of the two instruments with which I instructed you to cut two wounds.
And beware of cutting it except where it is right on the natural line; for if you cut beyond that, you will harm its parts.
And when cutting, use what I recommended in the cutting of two wounds, and treat it so that it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by cutting it, then use fire in cauterizing it without fear, and the way of cauterizing it is not except with sharp water; and it is that you place the head of the patient on your lap, press his tongue with the instrument I have said, and grab the water which I mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and apply the cause while alive, and pause neither thick nor thin, and fill the cavity of the instrument whose shape I have described.
Let the end of it be hollowed out in which the medicine is placed just like the cavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine over the nostril; and let the patient lean on his side so that saliva or spittle from his mouth does not fall into the nostril due to the medicine, so that no cause falls onto his armpit.
And with your hand hold the medicine and squeeze it over the uvula through a space of about half an hour until you see that it is blackened and subdued by the medicine's biting.
And if you wish, take a cotton swab and wrap it around the end of the nostril, and dip the swab in the medicine and insert the nostril with the swab in a tube so that the swab adheres to the tube; and do this as many times until you achieve what you want from cauterizing the grape, and then let it be dried out and fall off after about three or four days.
And if you see that the medicine needs to be repeated, then repeat it; and after cauterizing, clean around the grape with a swab dipped in wine, and cleanse it inside with what is in the circuit of the nostril from the medicine, and wash it with water rubbed with salt, and treat it outside with ointment, and inside with remedies until it is healed.
And this kind of treatment is safer than cutting and longer-lasting in terms of fear.
Sometimes the ulcer is cured by what is thinner than cauterizing and cutting, for it is cured by sub-fuming in this way: take chalk, hyssop, orris root, ruda, Arabic scinched, camomile, and brew them together with the others according to their kind, and add all of these causes into a wave, and boil it; and let the wave be laded with decent wax, and let there be a tube in the middle of the wave on which is placed an instrument made of silver or iron.
And let its end be inserted into the mouth of the patient so that the vapor reaches the uvula over the nostril, until the uvula is greatly obscured.
And repeat this over it several times until it is dried out; and beware of doing this while the food for the apostema comes out, for it will be too tight in the apostema, and you should not do that unless the apostema is cauterized.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then take a reed and attach a hole of horn to its end so that the mouth of the patient is bent; for the horn's hole prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
On drawing out thorns and what adheres in the armpit, and of other causes.
Sometimes thorns or the spine of a fish or another cause adheres in the armpit.
Then, if you wish to extract what is apparent from those parts, upon which the eye falls, press the tongue with an instrument before the sun, so that it may be revealed to you what is in the throat.
And that which is hidden and occult in the gullet, let it be caused to vomit before food has been digested in the stomach; for very soon the cause attached to the throat will be forgotten by vomiting. Or let the patient eat a piece of turnip or lettuce, or a mouthful of dry bread; or take a piece of dried sea-sponge and moisten it with bile, and then swallow it down, for when it reaches the place where the pain is located, it will be forgotten. And if you take an eel and moisten it with bile and let it be within the mouth and then gently extracted; or take a leek and gently insert it and gently pull it out again.
But if they are not forgotten by these causes which we have mentioned, then use an instrument of lead in this shape:
And let it be thicker than a small one, and at its end have a cavity which you may introduce into his throat with ease.
And let him keep his head elevated, and guard against touching the bone of his throat, lest he cause pain to himself or to the doctor with his hand.
But it is better that the doctor himself inserts it because of his knowledge of the location of the attached cause and presses it downward and pulls his hand upward with the instrument, all according to the amount that is prepared within him until it is forgotten.
Chapter XXXIX.
Of pains that are made in the members of the body. Of bleeding attached to the throat.
When bloodletting is performed with what we have said in the division of the cure with medicines and not with cauteries, consider then in the throat of the patient toward the sun, since you have pressed his tongue with an instrument as we have recommended.
Therefore, if you wish to see the bloodletting, draw it out with a needle that is very small or with a fine and decently sharp one.
And if it is not possible for you to draw it out with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the throat of the patient, and then pause in the concavity of the cannula a hot iron, and do this as many times (until the bloodletting has stopped and the pain leaves the throat) and abstain from water all day.
And then take a pitcher full of water and pour it over his mouth with that, and wash his throat with it; and do not swallow any of the water but spit it out into the pitcher again with your hand, for as soon as the bloodletting feels the water it will come out.
But if it is not forgotten by what we have recommended, then smoke the patient with camphor or asafetida through the instrument which we have recommended in the smoking of the uvula.
Do this, namely when it falls.
And there is another manner of operation in the smoking of it; namely that you take a wave in which are burning coals and the wave be covered with a cover on whose middle is a hole, and let the end of the instrument be fitted in the hole, then do the smoking from above. And let the patient close his mouth over the end of the cannula so that the smoking may not escape while you know that the smoking has reached the bloodletting because the bloodletting will soon fall.
And if it does not fall, then repeat the smoking several times and let the patient abstain from drinking, and eat salt causes and als, and do not drink water, and then necessarily this regimen will be forgotten.
And this is the form of the instrument with which bloodletting is drawn from the throat when it is seen; and it is like forceps thus you see except that in it there is some curvature: which is placed in the throat and its end is like a stork's beak which is gloved, in whose middle is tightened with a limed, so that when it presses any cause, that one does not slip.
Chapter XL.
Of pains that are made in the members of the body. In which is contained the sum of speech in perception of apostemas and in section of them.
The apostemas are of various species, from various manners according to what we remember of them, each in this book.
And they are diverse in penetration and section of them by two manners, one of which is according to the species of apostemas themselves; and the other according to what we perceive from moisture.
And the second manner is near to the sites in which they occur in the body, for the apostema that occurs on the head is another from the apostema that occurs in the buttock; and the apostema in the fleshy site is another from the apostema that occurs in a joint; and each of them is treated by operation.
And yet there are some apostemas which must not be penetrated but rather their maturity must be ripened or completed.
And yet other apostemas which must be cut when they are crossed and not ripened according to their completion; or apostemas that are near to the joints, for when apostema comes near a joint its nature is prolonged within while it presses what is in the circuit of it and very soon corrupts the ligaments or nerves of that joint, so that it makes a cause of contraction of that member; or apostema which is near a main member, if you delay the penetration of it until it has ripened unsoundness reaches to the main member; or apostema near the buttock, therefore penetrate it not-matured, and let not the perforation be cruel but rather that one which is near to the buttock so that its pressure does not make the membrane of it protrude and be elongated to the parts within the buttock, and a fistula be made or that one which is made in the end of it if it is not healed. But know the time of the penetration of the apostema and thus when it has ripened.
And know that it has ripened when there is the cessation of pain and fever, and then a decrease in heat and pulse, and the acuity of the head of the apostema, and the other signs.
And because know that penetration occurs in the site lowest of the apostema and pus coming from the cure of the eminence.
And penetration always be according to the length of flesh or bone, if the apostemas are in the parts of the feet or hands and in sites of ligaments, of cords of nerves, of arches; and, lastly, in all equal sites in which there is no reflection for in those in which it does reflect use penetration according to that member.
But when the apostema is in a fleshy site then it is better to let the penetration of it be according to what we have said so that its digestion may be confirmed according to completion for if you penetrate it before this, its membrane is prolonged and the tissue of it swells and very soon the lips of it are hardened and its depth.
But some apostemas are penetrated according to the breadth of flesh or body according to necessity, or according to that member of which it indiges.
And let you administer small penetration and in great apostemas great penetration or wide or many dry ones according to the difference of the size of the apostemas.
And others are pustules in which the skin is sunken and cut, when it is made like a loaf of bread and is made in a way that it has already been, as well as what happens in the case of swollen lymph nodes and boils.
And some of these are cut in a way that they have three angles; and others are cut in the shape of a myrtle leaf, just like the pustules on the armpits.
And some of them are given a round incision and a moon-shaped one, and they are similar to those.
But if it is not done like this with pustules that are superficial, you should penetrate it with a superficial penetration or just flatten it.
And when the pustule is large and has already collected pus, you should penetrate it and not be afraid to extract all of the content in that hour; you have extracted some from it, and then on the next day, you fill up the pustule again, and then you extract a larger portion, and then you do this several times according to the degree to which it has been absorbed.
And especially if the patient is of a cold disposition, or a woman in labor, or a small child, or an old person in decline, because the animal spirit is many times resolved by the release of pus, and soon after dies, so you should open them.
So keep this chapter with great caution, and then make your incision where you open these pustules, and cleanse the wounds and see if the wound of the pustule is small or a flat one; then you administer a few drops of oil or old wine. And if the pustule is large and the incisions are many, then you should apply a cotton pad in each hole so that they can absorb it.
And if a part of the skin is cut off or sunken in the circuit, then you should complete it with old oil or a thread made of linen cloth, just like when you treat wounds or roots, without moisture; and then tie it on the third day; and then process it and heal it with something that helps it heal.

But if it is in the winter, and the location of the pustule is a place of many nerves, then you should mix powders with wine and hot oil, and apply them to those places.
And if it is in the summer, and the locations are fleshy, then you should apply powders mixed with water and oil or wine and oil; and let this be applied until the third day, as we have said, and then open the pustule, cleanse it, and administer to its healing something that suits it until it is healed.

Chapter 52.
On the curing of an umbilical hernia.
An umbilical hernia is caused by several factors: either by fissures of the diaphragm which is located above the belly, or by a tear in the tissue or intestine, as already mentioned in other ruptures; or by blood that flows from a vein or artery, as previously stated; or by constricted bowel movement.
But if it is caused by a tear in the diaphragm and by an injury to the tissue, then the colour of the swelling will be similar to that of the rest of the body; there will be little pain, and it will appear in various places. And if it is caused by the egress of the intestine, then its location will be as we have described above, with greater or lesser intensity; and when you press it with your fingers, it conceals itself, and then returns; and it is very tender to the touch, and swells greatly with every movement of the body, and also due to warm baths and vigorous activity.
But if it is caused by humidity, then there will be little pain, and it will not conceal itself when pressed with your hand, nor will it constrict or burst.
And if it is caused by blood, then dark-coloured marks will appear on the swelling.
But if it is caused by a hernia of flesh, then there will be a large and hard swelling, as much as the amount of herniated flesh.
But if it is caused by bowel movement, then touching it will hurt.
And in performing the operation, you must first consider whether the umbilical hernia is caused by blood from an artery or a vein, or by bowel movement; for if this is the case, you are to abstain from treating it, as I have instructed you in the chapter where I discussed hernias caused by arteries and veins.
And if the umbilical hernia is caused by the intestine or tissue, then you must command the patient to lie on his back, and remain stretched out; then you are to feel around the entire circumference of the umbilicus, and command him to raise himself up with his arms resting on your knees, and you are to surround the area around the umbilicus with a palm's breadth all around the location which you have felt with your fingers; then extend the length of the swelling upwards towards the ribcage, and bind it tightly all around with strong thread or with twine, in a decent manner.
And then open the middle of the extended swelling, and insert your finger into that opening, and feel for the intestine; and if you have captured it or kept it in place with the thread, then you are to pull gently and push the intestine back inside the belly.
And if you have captured the tissue, then take it in your fingers, and cut away the excess.
But if it is contrary to an artery or vein, then surround it greatly and return to your operation; take two needles and thread them with strong thread, and insert two needles into the opening all around the hernia in a cross shape, so that they penetrate; then tighten the hernia at four points around the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and let the flesh return to its natural state and fall back by itself; or insert it when it has shrunk, and then care for it with a poultice so that it heals well.
And if the umbilical hernia is caused by a hernia of flesh or humidity, then you must hollow out the swelling as we have said, and draw out the blood or humidity which you have captured within it, and then care for it with what we have said so that it heals and solidifies.

Chapter .lv.
Of the cure of children whose foreskin is not pierced.
Sometimes, some children are born from the womb of their mother and the foreskin of the penis is not pierced (or it is tightly drawn or not in its place).

And with the spatula, pierce the foreskin at its middle. And put into the slit a slender pin of lead and tie that; and let it be for three days or for four.

And if you do not put in the pin, it is possible that the urine which goes over the place will not harm this closure.
But he of whom the slit is tight should be treated with a pin of lead, as we have said, for several days until it is enlarged.
But those in whom the slit is not in its place, they are hurt, which cause is that from them, he who is born and the foreskin is drawn down to the end of the penis so that he cannot urinate into the parts within, and there is no generation, because the semen cannot reach the mother according to rectitude, and it is a great illness.
And the manner of the operation on him is that the patient be turned onto his back, and then extend the foreskin of his penis with your left hand with violent stretching, and pierce the end of the foreskin at its tip with a sharp spatula, thus as a feather is pierced, or you scrape away any part, so that the foreskin comes to resemble the shape of the penis and the slit falls into its proper place according to what it should.
And beware of letting out blood in your operation, for often this happens; and let him take something that stops the bleeding, and cure the wound until it is healed.

Chapter 57:
Of the purification of children, and of the cure of those who are circumcised.
Circumcision is not the same as other wounds; rather, since it comes from our own action and will, and since its use is especially in children, we must consider in that case which operation and way is most quick and beneficial to health.
Therefore I say that the ancients did not prescribe circumcision to any of their freemen, for they do not use it in their laws, and it is only because we have acquired it by experience, which reason is that I have found most of the barbers and surgeons to use circumcision and purification with a razor and cauteries; and they use ointment with pitch and incision with the nail.
And if any wound of the nature of the body occurs to those who are circumcised, it behooves to cure them with a styptic and cauteries of hot iron; and if it is necessary, let a pointed cautery be done on the surface of the foot. And I have forbidden all these ways and not found a better purification with tweezers, or ointment with pitch, which is used in purification with the nail, because many times the membrane above has escaped, since the membrane of the foreskin has two tunics, and the inner one remains below, and it is easily forced to another incision and sure pain. And wounds which occur to those who are circumcised from the nature of their body need to be cured, for which reason these wounds are made; and if they cannot be cured with a styptic or cauteries of hot iron, let a pointed cautery be done on the surface of the foot. And by purification which is made with alpecat, which is digitalis, which surgeons use, the incision to the end of the virile member is not secure, because it soon enters its passage.
But in the operation with the nail, iron often obstructs and corrupts your operation. And the membrane of the infant is short by nature, and many times it happens in such a way that there is no need for purification, and I have seen this happen.
But of the purification which is made with tweezers and ointments with pitch, I have discovered the goodness by experiment, because the tweezers are of proportional incision, for this reason that the acuteness above is thus with the acuteness below; and therefore when you press your hand it is proportioned two acutenesses, and you make a cut according to a comparison in one time; and compression of the pitch is made with the figure containing the membrane of the penis throughout its whole part, with which there occurs no error at all.
And the manner of the first operation is that you make the infant be most upright if he is one who is a little bit small, so that you do not but that you smear the pitch on his penis only, and that you let him go until the next day.
And then let him be cheerful in all that way which is possible for you to receive his intelligence. And then let him be between your hands, setting out preparingly, and do not be careless; and conceal the tweezers in your hand or under your foot so that the infant does not see those or any other of the instruments.
And then put your hand on his penis and blow into the membrane, and draw that in so that the head of the penis is exited, and then purify that from what is gathered in it from dirtiness.
The form of the tweezers to purification is this form:
Let the ends not be cut and the others be cut, so that the point of the claw is not exited, and let the length of the two acutenesses be thus with the length of two handles equally.
But the error in purification is because the membrane which is inside entirely escapes, or it is separated from him after the incision.
Therefore you should lengthen that from your finger in front of the end of the place and that you make that according to equality.
And if you cannot hold it with your finger, then pull it out with tweezers and insert that instead.
But if three days have passed and the swelling has not gone down, then you should let the abscess burst so that the pus can come out easily, then scrape away the dead skin and insert something to aid healing.
And be careful with the head of the penis, for it should not be harmed.

And if it is cut below the foreskin and follows the natural lines, it will not be noticed.
And then treat the wound as we have said so that it may heal.

Then slowly pull the needle out with the wool, because the urine will follow the wool.
Then extract the needle and treat the urine, using the syringe; do not stop doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.

Chapter 62.
Of incision upon a protruding hernia.
A protruding hernia is nothing but an accumulation of moisture in the white membrane which is beneath the cords of the intestines containing the bowels, and it is called the ciphacus.
And in some cases it is in the upper membrane which nature has prepared in the part of the bowels, where there is a different bowel; and it is the cord of the intestine between the white ciphacus which we have mentioned, and it is not anything but fat.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness which happens to the intestines, because that matter is poured into them; and sometimes it happens due to a blow made upon the intestines.
And this moisture has many colors, for its hue is either inclining towards yellow or blood-red, or red, or it is dark and mud-like, or it is watery white, and that is what is most common.
And the signs by which one knows that this accumulation of water is in the white ciphacus which we have mentioned are these: for if it is in the white ciphacus, then the ulcer is round declining a little towards length with the shape of a bowel, and it appears on the intestine, for the moisture contains that of all parts.
And if the moisture is in the upper membrane, then the ulcer is round with the part of the bowel, and because of that one thinks that it is another bowel.
And if the moisture is between the cords of the intestines and the white ciphacus, then cut beneath the middle.
And when you want to know the hue of the moisture, then probe the hue of the moisture in the ulcer with a hot needle whose form is preceded; and according to what comes out on the track of the needle, judge what is inside it.
Therefore when we come to the cure of it with iron, then command the patient to be bled if that is possible for you, and warm the body of him.
And then let him lie on his back for some cause a little while, and put under him many cloths.
And then sit on the left side of him, and command the assistant to stand on his right, who extends his finger to one of two sides at the cords of the intestines and to the part of the navel.
And then take a flat needle and split the cords of the intestines from that middle place along for the length until it reaches the penis.
And the incision is made according to the directness equally distant from the line which passes through the hair of the intestines, so that it reaches the white ciphacus containing it.
And cut it, and examine, and watch out so that you do not push into it; and your cutting with which you cut it by part according to the amount that is possible for you to examine it.
And then pierce the full ciphacus of water with a wide incision, and extract all the water.
And then separate between two lips of the incision with pincers, and extend the ciphacus to the parts above; and do not touch the cords of the intestines containing it, and cut the ciphacus in that manner that is possible for you. And cut it in its entirety or in pieces, and especially the sides of it sottilely, for if you do not examine the incision of it carefully, there is no security that the water will not return.
But if the bowels process from that hole of its cords in the hour of your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the ciphacus, return it inside, and then sew together the two lips of the cords of the intestines, and then heal that as well as the other wounds, so that it is healed.
And if you find the bowels putrefied due to another disease, then lie the vessels which are on the suspensory, and extract the bowels, and then heal that as we have said.
But if the water is accumulated in two parts, then know that there are two hernias; therefore cut that part according to how you have done it in the other equally; and if it is possible for you to do one operation, then do it.
And then you should wipe away the blood, and put into the incisions wool which is submerged in oil or in rose oil; and lay another wool which you have submerged in wine and oil, and spread that over both intestines and the navel; and put over it folded cloths and lint, and tie those over with bandages according to that form:
and then put on two cloths and they are folded with wool which is dyed according to that appearance; and the extremities are made with which it is bound with a thread or with wet wool, and let them be joined with compression in the band of the trousers, and one of the extremities pierces the anus towards two, and the second pierces at the part of the foreskin, and two extremities over two thighs below and two extremities over two angles, and all compression is adjusted in the band of the trousers.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with cautery instead of cutting with iron; and you should take a sottile cutlass cautery and insert it into the groin, and ignite it according to our recommendation while the white capsule containing water is exposed; then you take the cautery in this way: it resembles greek letters.

And know that this operation is much safer and much more distant from blood flow, and better than an operation with cutting.
But if you are bothered by an abscess during cutting or during cautery, or by blood flow, then cure which ever disturbance occurs to you by that, with treatment according to what is said.

Chapter .63.
On cutting on a fleshy hernia and its treatment.
Know that in cutting on this hernia there is great fear of danger in the more common dispositions, because I have seen its dismissal and saving by that better way.

Therefore I say that abscesses arise in the parts of which the genitals are composed and are caused by several causes: either by acute sharp excess, which is distributed to the genitals, or by impact.
And its color is according to the color of the body, and it does not cause pain in that place, and it is a serious abscess.
And often it is a calcified abscess, of which the color is dusky, and there is no feeling in it.
And sometimes it is caused by knotting of the vessels and by inflammation of those as a result of which there is already perceived an impression of it, or by inflammation of the veins.
But that which is caused by inflammation of the vessels is known by the dispersion of the abscess when you compress it with your fingers; and it does not cause pain when touched.
But that which is caused by inflammation of a vein is not dispersed by any cause because of compression with the fingers.
And it is possible that on this species there be made a cut and on the other meats; and you should insert into the groin of the genitals, and open up the hollows above them, and extract the contents of the white capsule, and ligate the suspenders of the vessels, and insert the suspender further in so that you have emptied it of all its parts.

But if there is flesh growing in the place of the adhesiveness which is behind, then you should insert all that is in it and extract the hernia as we have said.
Therefore when your operation is completed, then fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine, and heal it with that other treatment until it is healed.
