All are ground in a mortar with a little moisture, and strained, and cooked with water or egg white, and administered.
Method of emplastrum for joints and bones separated from their places, and pain caused in them, and to fissures and fractures: take soft wool and submerge it in sweet wine and cut oil, and place it on the affected area.
Let this emplastrum not be used to restore strength, but to cauterize and expel pain properly.
Another method of emplastrum that heals broken bones: take leaves from a fig tree and leaves from wild poppies; pound all together and make an emplastrum from them.
Method of emplastrum administered for the restoration of broken bones and when you want resolution of the remaining of an abscess: take pounded marsh mallow, chamomile, and violet flowers, and flour of bran, all together in one part .I. Let all be ground and cooked in wine, if the affected member is not wounded.
But if it is wounded, then cook it with water of coriander or with water and use it.
Another method of emplastrum which is stronger in resolution than the previous one, when an abscess happens due to the restoration of a member: take pounded marsh mallow and sesame seeds, and ballota, and marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, all together in one part i unground; let all be ground and cooked with water of celery or with warm water or wine, according to the quality of the affected member and its rest of color.
But ipocras doesn't dry in its own free that it is placed on the broken member due to its restoration but wax made of wax and oil and no other cause; and return that it is between thickness and thinness.
But it is seen that they are placed on the broken member due to its restoration and the causes in which there is dryness with little heat are also like those causes that we have recounted.
Method of emplastrum that provides to injury and pain: take mace, cesium, human hair or bird feather, marsh mallow, and salt, all unground; let all be ground and strained and cooked, and make an emplastrum from it.
But the amount according to the quality that covers the bandage and then it is loosened, consider if there is no pain nor swelling, nor the bone is broken not moved from its place, then you do not displace for many days. And if it happens to the affected area violent pain or swelling or inflammation, then you cut or loosen it at the hour and remove the emplastrum from it.
And take a band of linen and marine sponge wet with warm water, and soak the affected area in tepid water, and wash it with that until the swelling and pain are reduced; then let it rest for an hour; then place on it soft wool soaked in sweet wine and oil or rose oil, and bind it up for one night within which you are sure of the abscess cauterized, and the inflammation of the member is soothed, and the pain of it.
Then repeat that gentle compression and little binding, and not tighten it by your first tightening, and apply that until it is healed.
And if the abscess and rogor and pain and inflammation and all the accidents are now reduced to all, and you have stopped using emplastrum and compression, then repeat that as you did at the beginning equally.
And if there is not any cause to the member of those that we have mentioned, then do not loosen it but after three days or four or five or seven. And some time all that is left for twenty days according to how you appear by the loosening of the bandage, as we have said, until the fracture heals, and approaches the coagulation of the flesh on it.
And then tighten the compression but not as in your first compression all together, and adjust in the swelling of the member, according to how it is preceded by its memory.
But if you see the fracture is already dried and wasted but not closed, then know that food is prohibited to reach it; and then soak it with tepid water due to total exhaustion that of the member; and let it be for three days; loosen the compression a little, and by this operation food reaches the member, and it is healed lightly.
But that which is done by the false restorators of the member's fracture due to its not being restored at the beginning, according to how it covers and is restored according to tortuosity, is error according to their operation, and great fear, and if they were stretched in their free parts and turned am on them.
And I have not seen any of them doing that, because it is more reasonable that it does not happen.
Chapter according to: Of fracture when it happens in the head.
The kinds of fractures are many, and their shapes are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for any fracture is not done by the impact of a staff, and the impact stands all the bone within the skull, which is under the skull, as it happens in the foot, and by this that kind of fracture is named ascial; or the staff penetrates a part of the bone, and penetrates the flesh of it and does not go through the interior of it; and this kind of fracture is named absolute elevation.
And the split of those two fractures or is great or small, and the fracture that is compression or atraction; and cause of it is impact by a stone or falling on stone, and to them similar.
And this fracture is penetrating that it approaches the membrane which is under the bone; or it is in the face of the bone, and the split of these two fractures or is great or small.
And in a fracture it is removed from the bone, in the subtlety of the hair's smallness, and because of this it is named this kind of capillary fracture.
And it is a fracture that is done by either chipping or stone percussion and their likes, causing some lamella of the bone to enter the cavities inside, and it is done in the concave place just as it happens to fingers when they are hit with a rock and there is an entrance made into the cavities within them.
And mostly this happens to the bodies of hollow bones, just like in baby skulls.
And for all these species of fractured sutures, they are separated as if with stars. And then I will have the memory of all this in my place.
And I know all these species of fracture by detection on those, and by inquiry of their feeling and appearance of rotten flesh that is upon them.
And capillary fracture is known by that being uncovered on the bone and being cleaned, and being stung over it, for then the fracture appears black.
But in the cure of fracture consider at the beginning the accidents of the patient, and then if you see by the accidents that signify by manifest signification for fear, as with vomiting of cholera, and extension, and loss of understanding, and absence of speech, and syncope, and acute fever, and sweat of those, and redness on them, and similar to them in the major dispositions, it is not healed.
And if you see accidents not occurring you hope for health then you begin the cure of it.
The reason for which is that if it has come to you the sick person in the beginning as it was sick and that is on the day twelve according to all disposition, then cover that you study in the appearance of the bone against the day fourteen.
And if it is in the days of summer then cover that you cut the bone against the seventh day having that which is below the bone be rotten by the membrane and that the accidents we have counted happen.
And if the fracture has already come to the membrane covering the brain and it is with small pieces and crushed then cover that you insert the crushed and ground piece according to what I will tell you.
And it is that you scrape the head of the sick person and uncover upon the bone according to habit and manner to you possible according to the figure of the plague and that it be lighter on the sick person.
And if it happens to you that you must make the detection of the bone and bleeding of pus or cauterization then you stop it with what covers, and you moisten the place with bandages infused in wine and rose oil and let the plague rest while the pustule is settled and blood stopped; and then proceed to the incision of the bone and removing it.
And that be according to one of two manners of operation.
And one manner is that you insert the bone with a narrow incision knife and this is its shape: And then administer another incision knife this one a little wider than that; and this is its shape:
And then administer even more the incision knife wider than the second.
And have that it be ought to you number of incision knives various, of which some are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than the rest and are in the thickness of their sharpness, and they are of iron or steel.
And use them with facility upon the incision knife so that it does not cut into the head and lead that one.
And if the bone is strong and hard then cover that you pierce into its circuit before administering the incision knives with awls which are called non-deep awls.
And they are not named thus but because those do not pass the end of the bone but the awl is round or rounded on its edge and below the head of it looks like margin or small circle, which forbids being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of those awls great number of which each one suits the thickness of each bone, within that it be to you present in all skulls the extremity of which is acute, the quantity in length and brevity according to the quantity of thickness of this skull.
And this is the form of three species of awls: The quality of boring into the circuit of the broken bone is that you pause the awl on the bone and rotate it with your fingers within that you know that the bone is already penetrated; and then change the awl to another location, and pause what is between every hole the quantity of thickness of the thread or wrap around that.
And then insert with incision knives what is between each crack of the bone and do that more subtle than you can on its end in fineness, as we have said, within that you erradicate the bone or with your hand or with some other cause by any tools which you will have apparatus for, as tongs and forceps subtle.
And have that you keep with all precaution that the incision knife does not touch any nerve of the brain.
And then when you have bored around the bone and separated it from the brain if it is continued with it, and have that extracted, cover that you scrape and gnaw it in its roughness of what is in the bone with another instrument similar to an incision knife but indeed let it be thicker and more subtle than the other incision knives.
And if you break any of the small bones and sutures then take that with facility by what is ought to you from the tools, and then cure the plague with poultices and ointments which we have said.
And the other manner of operation is lighter, distant from fear, which is said Gualia and is broken by great force; and this is its speech: it is necessary to you in the beginning that you begin to uncover the crack of the bone of the place in which there is the fracture most violent and most manifest, within that when you have uncovered that crack and you will have paused below it the extremity of this incision knife lente and gentle of which this is the shape:
And the lenticular crack is lion and does not insert any cause, and the acute crack is of it in two sides forward in length as you see; that the lenticular crack be attached to the brain by the membrane of it cutting into the bone.
And then pierce over the incision of one crack with a small hammer within that you insert all the bone with facility as it is turned and you be sure of the membrane that no accident happens in any manner which you fear in any way, and if the patient was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he was sleeping.
And then if it breaks any cause attached to the bone of the membrane in some place of bones, insert that of it with the extremity of the incision knife lenticular and deliver that of it with facility for it is delivered from it its harm and its fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate within the membrane and what is broken or remained of the bone is the membrane of it and you break into it and gnaw its roughness and sutures subtle, then cover that you scrape that roughness and erradicate those sutures with razors subtle by which you have prepared the number, of various quantities so that be to you possible administering them in every place most convenient of them and most proper of them according to what you are led in your operation that broken bone and the figure of it.
And whoever you administer to in your care, with which tools you extract the bones at the start of those wounds, and then use that tool on the pus beneath them, and do not stop doing this until you have extracted all the pus and the subtler humors from all these places.
But in any of the other small broken capillaries and subtle fractures, whoever you are treating should be cured according to what is lost in order to rectify it.
And it is a cause that has not been revealed above all those who have studied that art. And now about what we have written, we will show in great fractures.
And if the skin is stretched when you remove the bone, then take a piece of cloth according to the quantity of the wound and soak it in rose oil and place it over the hole of the wound; and then take another cloth folded or doubled and soak it in wine and rose oil and place it over the first cloth and do this gently so that you don't harm the nerve; and then administer on that a liniment, and don't tighten it except for the quantity of the wound.
And leave him with this treatment for a day or two until you are sure of cauterization; and then remove it and administer medicines that get rid of superfluous moisture, such as lint and barley flour, and powder of onions and of aristology, and of similar things to these.
And it is that you make of these medicines a powder that can be sprinkled over the wound as they are dried; and generally, administer to him all medicine of which its property is that it cleanses and doesn't bite.
And take care according to your virtue that the wound in your curing is clean; and let there be no surdity of ointment or other thing in it; nor let virulence gather in it; for when it is applied on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts it and causes evil in the patient.
And it occurs in the membrane of the head when the bone is removed and especially when the cure of it is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore if these accidents occur in the patient, know that he has six diseases.
And if blackness does not occur except from the medicine that is applied to it and it is in the virtue of that medicine that makes that blackness, then take a portion of honey and of rose oil [portions] iii. and with those lines a cloth, and then place it over the membrane; and then cure that with spices of cure that are suitable until he is healed.
Chapter iii.
Of restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two places, some more prominent than others, or one of those bent slightly, because there are two bones in it, for the lower part is cartilaginous and not broken, and nothing happens to it except curvature and tortuosity and sinusitis.
But if one of the two places is broken, then you should insert your little finger into his nose and grip that fracture inside with your index finger and push it outward with your thumb until the nose returns to its natural shape. And let this be done by you easily, and take care that he does not cry out from that operation on the patient.
And if the fracture is on the most prominent part of the nose and the finger cannot reach it, then let it be straightened with the tip of the needle in which there is a small thickness.
And if the fracture is on two places, then you should do that similarly.
And let him be restored on the first day of the fracture if it is possible; and if not, then let him rest for the cauterization of the wound for seven or nine days.
And then insert into the tip of the nose a piece of cloth if there is one fracture, or two splints if there are two fractures, and let the splint have enough thickness to fill up the gap in the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the splints should be wrapped in a cloth, and changed every day, but I have not seen this; others say that the cloth should be wrapped in a ball of honey comb with powder of moly; and then let it stay until the bone is set and the cartilage is hardened.
And in some cases, let the bone of the nose be inserted instead of splints, a feather of the peacock more robust that is wrapped in cloth of lion; and let it stay until the fracture of the nose is more violent and that the patient is not prohibited from breathing.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want to do it, then do it, and if you want to make splints, then make them.
And if another cauterization occurs on the nose, plaster it with ceratum or cotton soaked in wine and rose oil, or with diaquilinum.
But if there is no cauterization on the nose, then let you plaster it outward with sesame flour and powder of onions cooked in honey; and then place over it a sponge of lion's wool, and do not press the nose for any reason.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are crushed, then split them and remove them with tools that are suitable for those; and then sew up the cut, and cure it with what encapsulates and consolidates, and plaster it in whatever is convenient.
And if there is a wound inside the nose, then let you cure that with splints and administer lead tubes until it is completely healed.
Chapter iv.
Of restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the jaw is broken and there is no wound on it, observe then if the fracture is outward only and not broken in two parts and bent inwards, then its understanding is easier.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, then insert your left index finger into the mouth of the patient; and similarly if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert your right index finger; and push by that the curvature of the fracture inside with ease to the outward parts by your other hand from the outward part of the bone; by this he will be evenly straightened.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two parts, then let you administer extension of both parts according to its rightness until it is possible for it to be even.
And if there is concussion of teeth to disruption, then tighten those from which you expect that they will break with a thread of gold or silver or silk; and then place over the broken jaw ceratum, and then a doubled cloth, and over the cloth a great plate decently made, or a stone of only length of the jaw; and then tie that above according to how it is equipped to you as the application convenes so that it is not destroyed.
And command that he have rest and tranquility, and let him drink cooling drinks of lions.
And if you think that it is altered in some way, then let you bind and loosen it on the third day; and then rectify what is altered of it and plaster it with powder of moly, with honey comb, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the ceratum from it, and place over the plaster lion' s wool.
And then when he is lying still and that plaster is applied to him, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it, so that you do not disturb it until the fracture is healed and strong; for in many ways this fracture is tightened within three weeks.
And if another blister occurs from a cautery, then administer what we have said many times in the treatment of him until he is healed and that blister is resolved.
And if the fracture is complicated by an infection, then you should carefully examine whether the bone is separated or crushed; then you skillfully remove those crushings with that which is convenient in removing them with instruments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you widen it by phlebotomy according to the amount of your need.
Then when you have removed those crushings and they do not heal, then you clean the opening of the wound if it is large; and if not, place one of those plasters that are convenient for that and let it become embedded until it is healed.
Chapter 5:
Of the restoration of the collarbone when it is dislocated.
Many times the collarbone in front is broken by a blow from a muscle, and that fracture occurs in one of three ways:
Because it is either broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without the occurrence of blisters; and its restoration is easier; or it occurs with blisters, and its restoration is difficult; or the fracture is complicated by an infection.
And the operation on it, when the fracture is complicated by an infection, is that you have two ministers, so that one has the support and follows the collarbone that is broken, and the other extends the collar under that part.
Then fasten the fracture with threads so that it is made in the shape of a cone and not have a small eminence or concavity.
And if you need more extension, then cover the area under the armpit with a thick pad of cloth or wool, and let its size be according to the amount of your need; and extend and elevate the collarbone and contract the pad with your hand until you fasten the fracture as it should be.
And if you cannot draw the end of the collarbone to the outward parts because it is reached by the breast, then let the patient be reclined on his chest, and place a cushion over his shoulder, with a half-cushion in size, under the muscle of the collarbone.
And the minister presses the muscle of it to the outward parts below so that the collarbone bone is elevated from above, and then you correct the fracture and fasten it with threads.
But if you feel blisters on the collarbone and they are not resolved, then let them be lanced and those blisters extracted with ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you devise an incision of it with one of the incisers which you have prepared for that purpose, after you have placed below the collarbone the instrument that serves as the head; and it is an instrument of wood or iron, and this is its form:
And it is like a chisel of which this is the concavity; and let its breadth be according to what you need of it, according to the size of the bone or its thinness.
But its length be according to what is possible for you in the operation; and let it have two ends just as before, and one end be broad and the other narrow.
And if the opening of the wound which you have lanced when you have extracted the blister from the bone is large and it is certain that there will be a blister from cauterization, then you adjust the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the opening is small, or you fear a blister from cauterization, then you fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the amount of the opening of the wound.
And if a blister from cauterization occurs, then soak a cloth in warm vinegar and rose oil and place it over that.
And altogether, let the stake not be resolved over the broken bone; and therefore you see the patient every day, that when the ligament becomes soft or you see the stake already resolved, then you correct it and make the fastening strong.
And then place the patient's head upon his arm, and let there be placed under the armpit of him a little cushion; so that with that is elevated the support of it from the side of that, and therefore is elevated the fracture of the collarbone to elevation of the muscle; or let the arm be bound to the neck of him.
And do not remove the ligament unless blisters occur in the place definitely, or within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster when you have seen its face to that, and adjust in constriction and restore it and leave it until it is restored and the fracture of the collarbone is coagulated.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the moon in 28 days, and in some men it is made within a month of that time.
Chapter 6:
Of the restoration of the fracture of the shoulder.
The spatula is broken in the wide place of it and not broken from it except its margins.
And then when it is broken at the place of it — and it is broken in the middle place of it — and is not congealed except by contact, then according to the figure of the fracture you have the preparation of it and reduce that according to the natural figure in every way of engine which is possible for you.
And then place over the place powder of wheat with white of egg and piece of linen; and then place doubled cloth; and then place over it a wooden stake that is thin according to the quantity of the spatula all or perhaps a little.
But if under the stake is a concavity in any of the places of the spatula, then you fasten that concavity with piece of linen, and then place the stake according to equality.
And then strengthen above it with long ointment with decent constriction and with firmness of the stake so that it is not resolved in its place.
And consider the ligament every day; and whenever it becomes soft, ligate another time and tighten it and equalize the stake if it is resolved in its place.
And hold the patient upon his own side.
And the spatulate is restored in 20 days or less.
And when this number is complete, then you should dissolve the ligament; and know that it belongs to the bones from which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the place of the bone to the bottom of the cord, then you should split it, and ostiate it, and do according as we have said in the fracture of the collarbone of suppuration of pus if it happens here.
Chapter .vii.
On the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot sometimes is broken in one place and there is no pain therefrom; but the extremities of it are sore, and the bones are crushed and splintered.
And of its accidents when the middle of it is broken there ensues a decline to the parts within and there befalls it great pain, and difficulty in walking, and cough, and strong vomiting of blood; and a cavity is manifested in the broken bone and is not concealed from sensation.
And the restoration of it is that the patient should be reclined upon his back, and place under him a cushion; and press against the muscles of him, and adjust the ribs of him with the hands of two men, and keep him in the operation of it and in the setting according to every possible way that he convenes to it, until he returns the shape of the bone according as it was.
And then place upon him plaster and felt, and place over it a bed of subtle sawdust or of ashes, or of things like them in lightness, after you have enveloped that in cloth; and then carefully apply that against the broken bone lest it be hurt; and go with ligature according to roundness to the back sometimes, and strengthen that with decent tightness; and consider the ligature at every hour, and when it is softened strengthen that.
And if necessity requires the removal of that you must cut off that or press upon it, or pain, or suppuration that befalls it in the place, then you should cut and unbind that and eradicate the plaster and rectify that which befalls by way of cure of it; and then return the plaster, when you see is fit for it; and adjust the tightness on him until he is healed.
Chapter .viii.
On the restoration of the ribs when they are splintered.
Know that fracture of the ribs does not occur except in gross places that follow the back; and from the ends of them to the front there befalls to those no constriction except by reason that they are cartilaginous. And cognition of it is not concealed from sensation you must make inquiry of it with the fingers.
And the restoration is that you should close the fracture with the fingers according to possible manner, until it is healed according as it was, and then plaster and strengthen the broken bone with splints, if he needs them.
And if the fracture of the ribs is declining to the parts within then there befalls to the patient violent pain and piercing, just as piercing that befalls that which has been punctured, and by reason of that the bone thrusts upon the membrane; and there befalls to him difficulty in breathing, and cough, and vomiting of much blood; and this is of difficult cure.
And now the antiseptics are applied to it with warm poultices.
And of them I say this: let him abstain from foods that engender inflammation and windiness, so that the belly may be swollen and extended, and the fracture be driven to the parts without.
But we would abstain from it lest it be the cause of suppuration to generate cautery, if not happen; and if it does happen then it would help.
And some of them say that it should be laid upon with fomentation and then drawn with strength; and this is like ratiocination, but indeed it is feared that the fomentation would drive out the superfluities to the place by reason of weakness.
And some of them say: let the place be covered with wool that has been dipped in hot oil, and let there be laid in between the ribs a poultice which is moistened, so that the ligature is wrapped around it according to roundness; and then let the patient be cured by cure of pleurisy of food and medicine.
And if it causes violent constriction that he does not endure, and the bone thrusts upon the membrane with piercing pain, then fear about the patient; therefore let him be cut over the place, uncovered of the splintered rib, and then let there be placed over it an instrument that conserves the ciphac, the memory of which precedes, and that we insert into the bone with ease, and extract that.
And then let us adjust the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with cauterization, and cure that by way of plaster until it is healed.
And if suppuration befalls within then you should cut and apply poultices in warm oil, and let it be cured by what covers the suppuration within.
And let him be reclined upon his side upon which he slept until he is healed.
Chapter .ix.
On the restoration of the fracture of the vertebra of the back and of the neck.
Sometimes fracture befalls to the bones of the neck, and it extends to their articulations, and likewise to the vertebrae of the back. And if it befalls to anyone and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not, then consider, for if you see the fingers are already swollen and stupefied and dead and cannot move those nor extend nor tighten them, and when you pinch those with your fingers or stab those with a needle and he does not feel pain in those, then know that he is not healed, and in months of disposition it is mortal.
And if you constrict those with the fingers and stab those with a needle and he feels pain in those, then know that the marrow of the bone is saved, and that the patient will be cured by cure.
And if you see the vertebrae of the neck like that and wish to know whether he will be healed or not, then consider his feet.
Now if you see that they are already swollen, suppuration befalls to them like that which befell the fingers, and then when he lies upon his back he experiences windiness and loss of his will, and sometimes is reclined over his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then know that it is mortal, and therefore you must not work in the cure of him.
But if none of those befalls to them, then the cause is better and likewise less painful.
And the cure of him is that you pierce to the suppuration of the cautery that rests upon the splintered vertebra with oil which is warmed, or with balls of hempseed which are scorched; place over it sometimes during the day while the suppuration is settled.
And then place one of the poultices that are comforting and hot, and strengthen over it with ligature; and command the patient to take rest and tranquility, and not sleep upon the part from which he experiences pain until he is healed.
And if suppuration befalls by reason of the articulation of the bone or any cause that is already separated from him, then cut over it and let that bone dry; and then adjust the two lips of the wound with suturing if it is large; and cure that by way of poultice of the intestines, until it is healed.
But if the lowest of the vertebrae is splintered and it is the end of the spine, then let you insert the tip of the middle finger of the left hand into the anus, and close the splintered bone with the right hand according as it is possible by second position; and then place over it plaster and splints if they need that, and then strengthen that.
And if you feel broken bones in him, then you will put a splint on it, and fix it, and heal the wound with the medicine that is preceded so that it may be healed.
