Chapter XII.
On the restoration of fractures of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called the two bones of the elbow; one of which is small, and is that which is above the bend of the wrist, and the other is large, and is placed below the small bone, from the part further down.
And usually the larger bone only is broken, or the smaller one, and rarely are they both broken at the same time.
And when the smaller bone is broken at the top, then its restoration is more difficult, and its healing is even more so.
And when the bone is broken below the elbow, then its fracture is harmful, and its healing is difficult.
And worst of all is when both bones are broken at the same time.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller one at the top, then it is appropriate for the doctor, in performing its restoration, to apply a small extension with ease while that part is bandaged.
But if the larger bone is what is broken, then it is fitting that he apply more vigorous extension.
And if both bones are broken at the same time, then you must apply extension with the greatest force.
And you must make the hand into the shape of a hand when performing its restoration and extension by extending it over a cushion, with the palm of the hand upwards, more than all the others, and let the auricular part be further down than all the others, and let the patient be seated upon a raised pillow.
And let there be a cushion beneath him, in the height or angle of his body, so that he does not move about.
Then the assistant should extend the arm at the part furthest down with his hand or with a bandage, and another assistant should similarly extend it upwards.
And the doctor should bandage the bone as he reduces it to the best figure that he can.
And if in the setting of the bone there are any knots, then you must reduce all pressure in its place with your strength.
And if there appears in it a separated knot that punches through the tendon, and it is not within your power to restore it, then you must work on it and prevent that according to the manner which we have said above.
But if the fracture is compound, I have already spoken about that in this chapter, and so you may take its cure.
And if suppuration occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then you should spread a cloth soaked in oil of roses and white wax, made medium between thin and thick, and tightly bound over that suppuration; and then remove the cerate and apply plaster prepared from powder of moly with honey, and then place splints; and let the splint which is placed over that fracture be more layered and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm are seen in the greater dispositions, or whether the fracture is in one bone or both bones together.
And then apply your binding over the site of the fracture more strongly and more vigorously, and when you loosen the binding at the parts above the fracture or at its lower parts, loosen the binding a little according to how it is remembered in the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths which are wrapped over the fracture should be soft moist cloths, and not hard or rough; and the thread with which it is bound should be of an appropriate middling thickness between thin and thick, as we have recommended.
And consider the member and the binding after a few days. And if there is any cause for which rectification is necessary, then as soon as that happens to the member, then you should immerse the member in warm water until that subsides.
And let the member not be bound while it rests, and then return the binding.
And if the binding is already loosened and the bone is already set right and similar to those, then you should rectify all that with your strength.
And consider more if the nourishment is prohibited from coming to the member due to excess of binding, then you should loosen that a little and let it remain for a few days so that that comes to that member, and then bind that more strongly.
But if there is no cause to the patient for those which we have said, then it should not be unbound until after about 20 days or more of those.
And then let the patient's hand be suspended by its strength in an agitated movement, and make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or in 32 days, or very seldom in 28 days, and all that is according to the disposition of the complications and the dispositions of nature.
Chapter XIII.
Of the restoration of the wrist and fingers.
Know that to the wrist and knuckles of the fingers there often occurs fracture, and not to them but much movement many times.
And when it happens that there is a break or tear in the hand, then you should have the patient lean forward and lie down according to his ability, and then place your hand over that extended area.
Then the assistant should extend the broken bones and the doctor should bind those areas which are appropriate with a good bandage.
And then you should place plaster and padding if there is no abscess, and then put a poultice over it according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped that in cloth.
But if the cut is on the sides of the fingers and within the palm, then make a tent with cloth, and instruct the patient to hold that area with his palm which has been cut; then have it bound with long cloth.
And let the poultice of meat be applied to the palm with cloth, and have it bound according to how it should be.
But if the cut is on the outer parts of the fingers, then you should place poultices over it and another one below in the palm so that the hand can be opened and straightened; and then apply the binding as the hand turns around with the palm facing upwards between the thumb and fingers.
And if a cut happens to one of the joints on the fingers, then it should be bound according to how it should be and then bound with the palm; and if you want, place a small straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not bruised.
But if the cut is on the other fingers, thus it is in the middle finger, or in the index finger, or in the ear finger, or in the ring finger, then it should be bound and tied with that finger which has the cut; or they are all tied according to continuity, and that is better; or place a small splint over it as we have said in the pous.
And consider in your restoration and then that, the disposition of abscess cauterization and opposition — with your operation — with what occurs when any cause of those happens, according to how it is iterated in his narration.
Chapter .xiv.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and it is declared to the feeling according to how it turns to the front and to the upper part.
And its restoration is done in such a way that the bandage is bound over the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient lies on his back and on his face.
And then the assistant should extend all the bandages to their ends according to quality, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is near the hip joint, then let the bandage be near the groins at the end of the pubic bone, so that the extension pulls upwards, and let the other bandage be below the fracture.
And similarly if the break is near the groin, then let the bandage be made near the groin, and let the extension pull from below.
And then the doctor should bind the bone with both of his hands within in order to reduce it according to its natural appearance and the bone is joined by good conjunction; and then you should place plaster and binding if there is no abscess in the limb. But if an abscess happens in it, then let it be left for some days until the abscess has subsided, and then return to your cure.
The binding of it should be done thus: cover the fracture with a hard bandage, and fold that two or three times, and remove from it superfluity; and then wrap the leg so that it comes to the ankle and to the hip joint.
And put in long wool between the thigh and the leg near the groin at the ends below, and let the extremes of the wool come from the highest part of both parts, and then turn around on the leg and the thigh that which is superfluous from the bandage.
And then place splints over the thigh at the site of the fracture, and place one of those splints over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the emptiness between the leg and the thigh with soft cloths so that the binding is coagulated.
And then begin with strong binding of the middle over the site of the fracture with three or four wrappings, and as you distance yourself from the bandage of the site of the fracture, your binding should be lesser and more loose and more soft.
And then extend the two extremes of the wool to the ends below so that it comes to the ankle of the foot, and bind both extremes of the splints on the other side so that the bandage is not pulled from its place.
And then let the binding be over that, where it is so that no corrosion nor abscess nor inflammation happens to it and its likes; and if any of those happen, then treat him with removal of it, and correct what happens altogether, according to how we have said many times.
But if in the bone there is an infection of the bone, then you should bind it according to how you are able; and if not, leave it and remove that, and cure with what from which preceded is the reminder so that it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is bound by others so that they are fitted to it, thus as we have said in the assistant and in the arms.
But in the restoration of it so made, let the patient not have a limp; and if it is restored alone, then let the other leg be applied to it, then it is necessary that he limps from which one there is a fracture.
And know that the thigh is bound for 40 days or a few months or a few months, and all that is according to the diversity of the complexities and of the other dispositions.
Chapter .xv.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the groin.
Know that a fracture of the groin starts inside, but it happens to it many times traction.
But if a fracture happens to it, then it is not anything but crack or tear in its parts, and that is with wound or wounds, and over that all is felt by the sense.
And its restoration is thus that it is bound where it is broken in its parts with the fingers so that it is added and joined according to how it is possible by accommodation and ease and fabrication.
And then place plaster and place over that a round splint if there are indigent from that; and bind above it with binding as is convenient.
And then consider all the dispositions which we have commanded in the other fractures, thus as there is abscess cauterization and similar to those in that there is a stop to all accident with what to it is convenient so that it is healed.
Chapter .xvi.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the leg.
Know that in the leg there are two bones of which one is large, and it is named by name of the leg; and the other is slender, and it is named tibia.
And it happens to each of the species of fracture that it happens to both bones of the arm, and for that reason is done restoration of it thus as restoration of the arm is bound, and the operation of it is one.
And if are broken two bones together, then the leg is turned to all parts.
And if the more slender bone is broken, then the leg turns to the front parts.
If the bone is cracked, you treat it by bleeding, which is one of those things that are not your business.
Then administer extension and alignment and liaison of the bones according to the treatment.
But if the fracture of the leg is open and has many small wounds, then the extension should be lesser and gentler, and you must use your skill.
And if the leg has any cause for operation on the arms, and it is when you treat the bones and complete all your operation, then you will take weights of wood which are placed under the causes that are placed in soles, and they are made from white of palm; you arrange those whose small thickness is small, and there should be no gap between them, and their length according to the length of the leg, and into the deepest part.
Then wrap around each one in two wraps according to the length of it, and place one at the end of the leg on one side and the other on the other side, and they should be into the deepest part of the bottom of the foot.
Then tie the two bones in three places with two extremes and at the middle place, for with this tension the leg is prohibited from tilting to the right and left sides, and it stands in good position.
And a brace of wood has been administered according to the length of the leg, and it should be placed on it so that it conserves its movement.
And most importantly, you do this in fracture, and when it is properly healed.
And then consider the leg every day and study it with that previous study, and you must guard it from abscess and inflammation or similar causes; and when any of those causes occur, then apply to it what is necessary so that it becomes healthy.
Chapter .xvii.
Of the fracture of the ankle and the bones of the foot and the toes.
The ankle does not usually fracture completely; but the bones of the foot fracture sometimes; and few toes of the foot fracture, and they do not fracture unless there is attrition in the places of disposition.
And if the bones of the foot are fractured and those bones happen to be elevated upon each other, then the patient should put his foot on the ground prepared in this way.
Then place one foot on that which is elevated of those bones, and then press and heal those within so that they return to their places.
Then apply plaster and a plug on top of it, and place under the belly of the foot a small table to which are two pegs.
Then strengthen that with the foot with decent tension, because you have wrapped it with drapes and other things from which you are not able to get away.
And when three or four days have passed, then you loosen the ligament, for you will find the bones healed, or cracked, or dislocated, and the operation in those is just as you know it to be.
But if any toes are cracked, then restore those, and heal those just as you and your opinion are accustomed to in the restoration of the toes of the hand. Then place a splint on the cracked toe according to the length of the toe, and let there be more of it; and under the foot this table which is recommended to you, and strengthen that with decent tension.
But if that which is cracked of the bones is two or three or more, then you place on each toe a splint of its quantity wrapped in lion drape, and strengthen the table under the bottom of the foot, and let it stand on one of the pegs of the table so that it has good retention.
And it is convenient for you not to forget that you do not consider all the causes which are told to you in other fractures of the accidents that we have said; and apply to every accident what is necessary.
Chapter .xviii.
Of the fracture of the groin and the bone of the hip and the rod of man.
When the groin of the femur is fractured, then make that fall into the hollow, and then incline that to the side at the top a little, and let it be held behind.
And then the midwife fills the crotch with cotton, and let it be done in the crotch just as sperma; and then the femur is concussed, and elevated at the top little by little, for all that is forgotten within so that it becomes before the crotch just as sperma and the bone cracked returns.
And then sprinkle powder over the top of it, and when he wants to urinate, let him hold the crotch with facility and then urinate, and reduce and return that according to the manner according to which you measured it at the beginning, and then return to its first powder.
And do this for seven days or around those, for it is restored.
And if you want, take an ovela bladder and strengthen over the hole of it a straw of reed; and insert the whole bladder into the crotch of it, and then insufflate through the straw with strength so that the bladder is inflated inside the crotch, for the fracture returns.
And then let it be filled with sperma and stand for some days according to what we have recommended, so that it becomes healthy.
And when the bone of the hip of man or woman is cracked, then administer in its restoration and healing what we have recommended in the bone of the hip.
And that which is right is not hidden in these nearby fractures and which hardly happens, mostly to him to whom there is some study and use and understand my free will; for that fracture indicates to you in the greatest dispositions directly in the restoration and in the tension of it, and therefore understand well.
But in the rod of man when it is cracked, take the skin of a horse, and put the rod into that; and then apply over it wrap of drape, and let it be wrapped, and then let it be left for three days or around those, and let it become healthy.
Chapter .xix.
Of the restoration of the bones when they are with plague.
It is convenient for him to whom fracture happens with plague, and especially if the bone is large just as it is the bone of the head or of the helper, or similar to that, that you take care and fleubotomies that just as the conditions of fleubotomia testify to you so above.
And if the plague flows and bleeds, then it is necessary that you take care of the retention of it just as you pulverize over it a trissat zegi, unless another cause is present.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not prolong it, if an abscess does not occur.
But if an abscess occurs cauterized, then you let the restoration of it go within the sixth day, until the abscess is healed, and do not approach it on the third day and the fourth of all, for you will make bad accidents occur.
But if the cracked bone is prominent over the uncovered tendon, then it is necessary that you reduce and heal that just as with your hands with facility and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the position of it with your hands, then you reduce that just as with your hands with this tool; and this tool is made of iron, the length of which is seven digits or eight, and its breadth according to the quantity of the plague; and it is necessary for the physician who takes three or four according to the quantity which induces that in the cure in all kinds of fracture.
And it should be turned inside out, and there should be little roughness so that it is not folded over when the pressure is applied during the operation.
And it should have an acute tip to which is attached at its own ends; and the top of it should be thick, and half of it should go into the incisions.
And this is the shape of it:
And it is called in Greek "labra volant atelarem", which means a small spatula.
So that when the end of it is placed on the prominent end of the bone, it is elevated above it.
And it pushes the bone into a hollow, and then return the bone to its normal position with some traction.
And then try to bring the ends of the fracture together, and if the broken end is thin, and does not take the instrument well because of its small size, then you must insert that bone end so that the instrument can do its work.
But if you are unable to reduce the bone at all with what we have recommended, then cut it with one of the cutting instruments as best you can.
Then smooth away any roughness and small bumps on the bone.
And when the bone is reduced, and suddenly the patient feels great pain while being bent, then you know that the bone has not been returned to its natural position; and then do what you know will benefit the patient greatly.
When you have finished restoring the bone, then immerse it in black styptic wine, and especially if it is summer, and do not apply a poultice or any other cause which will bring about swelling and corruption.
And then administer stars in the hour of your completion of the restoration of the bone, and leave the wound uncovered so that you may insert the hook with tweezers according to the amount of the wound.
And be very careful not to strain the wound with the fracture, for many times foolish doctors have done this and caused death or corruption or putrefaction in their patients, which is unsanitary. And if it seems loose, soft, different from the other fractures.
And if the wound is large or is a bad wound, and you fear any of those bad accidents which we have mentioned, and pain occurs in that place, then do not put stars on it, but make an envelope of hard rags in place of stars and strengthen it with those.
And when about a day or two has passed and the wound begins to suppurate, then you must remove the cloth which you had placed over it with wine, and then administer poultices, according to our custom is that we treat wounds, also with tetrafarmacon poultice and similar to that.
And it is good for you to loosen the bandage and examine the wound every day morning and evening until it is consolidated and closed, and that the limb be prepared in such a way that the swelling runs from it into the surrounding areas with ease.
If after many days the wound is not consolidated or the suppuration has ceased from it, then you know that here there are small problems so that wherever you touch the wound, it hurts. And of those small problems which are separated and extracted, those from which the member is not separated and which cause pain when pricked, pierce them according to what is possible for you.
And if suppuration or redness occurs to the wound, which are stamen, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, then do not place on it any of those from which the cure is preceded, a narrative of which is given in its chapter.
And of that from which you must not be ignorant and make your prediction: when a large bone is broken, it protrudes above the member like the bone of the thigh or the helper, and alike to those of the large members, and do not presume traction on it, nor its extraction for many times death has occurred because of this; let it remain as long as it pustules, for it will fall off by itself after about 20 or 30 days. And then cure the wound if it is seen to you, and if not, leave it.
Chapter .XX.
Of the cure of nodules which occur in the path of a fracture.
Many times this nodule occurs in the path of healing of a fracture, and mostly it is near the joints, and it is irritated by its own action; and very often the member is prohibited from its own operation.
Then consider if the nodule is recent and administer to it medicines which have astringed like aloes, and senna, and mallow, and plantain, and like these; in such a way that you take some or all of them, and mix them with styptic wine, or white wine, or sour wine, and apply this to the nodule on a piece of cloth, and constrict it on it with good constriction.
And let the constriction be as long as possible for many days; and then loosen it and repeat another, until the nodule is swollen; or strengthen on it a sheet of lead descantingly, for lead has property to soothe all that is inflamed in the limbs.
But if the nodule is already turned to stone and tight, and necessitates causing it to be split open, then cut it along the highest part of suppuration, and insert the excess and prominence, or cut it with some sharp instrument until it is swollen; and cure it as it heals.
Chapter .XXI.
Of the restoration of a fracture when it is restored and what happens then to the member if it is thin and weak according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is restored, and then what happens to the member if it is thin and weak, then this is not done except for several causes: one of which is the abundance of suppuration of the wound and the ligature of it not according to nature; or by excess of swelling of the ligaments through which nourishment is prohibited from flowing to the member; or by abundance of heat; or by excess of movement not in its time; or by weakness of blood in the patient's body and of debility.
And the cure of this is to cause the patient to eat and make his heart tender so that blood is multiplied in him; and administer bath; and put gum and lettuce over it.
And then apply poultice over the member, so that the poultice attracts nourishment to it, or continue fomentation of it with warm water, until nourishment flows and returns to its natural figure.
Chapter .XXII.
Of the cure of bones broken when they are restored bent and prohibited from their own operation of what it is necessary for.
When it happens to the member already restored that healing of bentness and eminence occurs to the broken bone and nodule, and is fed from its form, but nevertheless the member is not prohibited from its own operation, then do not accept the word of him who examines this member that it is newly broken.
But now are many foolish doctors and restorators who have done this in our land; and this operation is very blameworthy having presented great fear, the sign of which is deadly.
But if claudication and nodule are recent, then let them be fomented with warm water in which they put soft herbs, as well as the leaves of mallows and their roots, and mallow, and like these; and let an ointment be made from it with emollient ointments, or let it be pressed with white poplar bark and be applied to it; or let it be bandaged with a poultice of poplar bark and be bound to it; or let it be pressed with fat of stearic acid and be bound to it; or let the fat of stearic acid be mixed with oil of camphor, and be applied to it.
And sometimes it is dissolved by frequent friction, however light, with the hands wet, and motion of the affected part in all directions at all times.
But if the tortuosity has progressed further and become strong and turned into stone, and is causing pain that demands treatment with iron, then let the pus above be opened and the continuation of the bone be dissolved, and that which is superfluous in the nodules of that joint with the bone be cut away with thin instruments, and let ease be administered to it by solicitous care and study.
Here is Chapter 23:
Of speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints of its place, by which motion is prohibited, and the form of the limb is changed, and it causes great pain and serious injury to the patient.
Therefore when any dislocation happens, it is necessary to reduce it immediately, and not delay in reducing it, for if it is delayed, the joint becomes calloused, and it is made very difficult by this to reduce the dislocation.
And for that reason it is not good to be pressed upon or stretched in the hour of callousing, for often it causes spasms and pains in them.
But when it happens, then you should have recourse to phlebotomy on the patient, and let the callous be left inside while he rests; and the limb should be fomented with tepid water and oil, and then reduced with ease.
And every limb be treated by that from which its memory will return to its place.
And you have already and orderly arranged the chapters on dislocation according to what is preceded in fracture, from the highest part of the body down to the lowest part.
Chapter 24:
Of treatment for dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are dislocated if not bitten; and the dislocation of both is made in one of two ways: they are either pulled from their places by slight motion and slightly softened, or else dislocated throughout and completely, so that they are drawn back at the joint where saliva flows from the mouth of the patient, and he cannot hold it nor close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped around his words.
But when its dislocation is slight, then in the greater dispositions it returns by itself for slight cause.
But if the dislocation is entire and complete, then let reduction of it be administered slightly, and not be delayed in any way.
And the minister should hold the head of the patient, and let the doctor insert his finger from one hand into the root of the dislocation of the mandible inside the mouth of the patient, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he inserts both fingers together, if the dislocation is on two sides, and the other fingers of his hands are outside holding them open; and command the patient to cease moving his mandibles and to relax those in every part; and the doctor should hold the mandible and press it in while it returns to its place.
And if reduction of it is difficult, and especially if both mandibles are dislocated together, then administer evaporation with tepid water and oil, so that reduction of both be made slightly and not delay the reduction of both in any way, as we have said.
And when they have returned and are closed, and the mouth of the patient is not softened, then pause upon them powders of linen with cerot, made from wax and rose oil; and let it be bound up with ease by mild ligatures.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and let his head be held between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right nor to the left; and do not force him to chew for any cause; having his food be meat of lion, so that when the pains are increased and the mandibles are swollen, then let him chew what he will.
And let that be administered with ease, and do not work upon the opening of the mouth after eating and drinking and waking, so that the mandible is swollen and healed.
And if reduction of two mandibles is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and not returned to their places, then often it happens that fevers and constant sweating occur, and very frequently the belly of the patient is swollen, and he vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is fatal; and often the one to whom it happens dies within seven days.
Chapter 25:
Of reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and of the extremity of the muscle.
Collarbone is not dislocated from within throughout its continuation with the shoulder; and sometimes it is dislocated at the part that is outward, and that is declared in sensation.
And its restoration is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. And then compress the joint with your palm with strong compression, for it will return.
And then pause upon it poultice and powders, and bind that strongly.
But the extremity of that which touches the shoulder and is continued with it, is not dislocated in any day; then let it be reduced and closed according as we have said and as it is prepared for you.
And then pause upon it poultice, and powders, and constriction, and command the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And with this method of cure is reduced the extremity of the muscle when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26:
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated at the part of the shoulder joint to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated at the part above the muscle, and that is done suddenly; and it is not dislocated at the parts behind by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated at the parts in front by the nerves.
And most often it is dislocated and drawn out at the parts below by the shoulder joint, and most often in those of which the flesh is thin, it is dislocated easily; but in those of which there are many meats, it is drawn out with difficulty.
And very often it happens to certain men by impact or blow, and the muscle of him is calloused with callous, and it is injured for that which has already been dislocated.
Therefore let you know this while you are standing over him, and then proceed in the cure of him.
And the dislocation of it is known when it is at the parts below by the shoulder joint; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the intact muscle, for you will see a manifest difference; and you will feel in the head of the muscle concavity and above the shoulder joint from the touch of the head of the muscle as though there were a hole, and the patient cannot reduce his hand to his shoulder, nor move that at all.
If the foot or ankle is dislocated, you will feel a clear bump and it will not be hidden.
And from this dislocation, there is an easier reduction when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the minister lifts up his hand to the parts above; and then he places his fingers under the patient's chin, and lifts up the joint with strength into its place, and the minister lifts up his hand, and extends that so that it returns more easily.
But if it does not return through what we have said, and so from the beginning that the dislocation occurred, after several days, then let the patient be bathed in warm water and let it be anointed with a liniment that softens, just as though his skin were razor-burned, with anise, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and let it be administered.
And then the patient is laid on his back and a wool pad is placed under his chin, held between elevation and compression, and then the doctor places his heel on the sphenoid bone, and lifts up the top of the muscle with strength, and pulls the patient's hand so that it is not burned at the parts below, and another minister holds the top of the patient's head so that it is not burned at the parts below, for as soon as this is done the joint will return to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner: and have a man who is taller than the patient stand on that side, and place his hand below the patient's chin and lift up the chin towards the parts above while the patient is suspended in air, and another minister pulls the patient' hand from the parts below his belly. But if the patient is light, then let him be suspended by another cause, for the sake of gravity, for the dislocation will return at once.
And it is reduced in the same manner: and it is that a long pole be placed on the ground, the top of which is round in shape, like a mortar, neither thick nor slender, and then let the patient be placed under it; and then let linen be placed over the pole, and the patient stand according to the length of the pole, and then let his hand be extended towards the parts below the other part; and let his body be extended from the other side with strength, for the joint will return to its place quickly.
And if it is difficult to reduce it by all the causes we have said, then use this cure: and it is that you take a pole of two cubits in length, and four digits in breadth, and its thickness is taken from the chin according to this form:
And then over the round head let linen be tied so that the pole does not hurt the patient.
And then let the patient's chin be placed under it, and his hand or arm extended over the pole towards the parts below, and let the pole be tied to the assistant's waist, and his arms and the end of his hand.
Then let the arm be placed on a rung of a ladder according to its breadth, and the hand extended towards the parts below, and the other body suspended from the other side, for the joint will enter at once.
And when its medicine is complete according to whatever way it is possible for you, then let you place under the chin a sponge of equal quantity; and then place plaster prepared from mill-powder with honey and egg white over all the muscle, as though it were rolled upon it, and then press down on the chin with decent tightness; and let the bandage be rolled over the plaster, and his hand be attached to his neck, and let him lie, and not hurt his hand for seven days.
And let the food of the patient be reduced a little, while the member is comforted, for it is easier to heal him.
And then let his food be increased after seven or five days and let exercise be given to him; and if it is firm and not soft, then he has healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times by moisture that occurs in him or by another cause, then let it be cauterized with three cauteries, as was said before in the chapter on cauteries.
And if all of this is done and then after seven days the bandage is removed and the joint is not firm, and the plaster and tightness are reduced upon it several times, and it is not firm, and can't raise his arm to the parts above, then know that its nerves which are at the top of the muscle have been cut or extended or made soft, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
From the dislocation that is made to the feet and the breast or the back parts, its reduction is done by pushing and extending with hands, as it returns.
And let the other tightness be administered upon it as it is healed.
And if after the healing the heaviness increases upon the member and the movement of it delays, then let the patient take a bath several times so that this heaviness is eased and he returns to his first nature.
Chapter .XXVII.
Of the cure of dislocation of the neck.
Know that the joint of the neck is dislocated with difficulty and returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated to all parts, and especially to the front part more than the back part, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because it hangs down below the shoulder or below the touch according to whatever figure it is dislocated, and when you adjust the neck that is dislocated with the head, this is declared to you manifestly, for the joint is hollowed out and cannot bend the arm, nor touch with that the muscle itself.
And let you know that your neck and reduce the dislocation at once before an abscess comes upon him with a cautery, for if an abscess comes upon him with a cautery, it is difficult to reduce it, and it is not healed of all; and especially when it is at the back parts, for it is the worst of all kinds of dislocation, and of greater pain, and many times they die from that.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce, is that his hand be extended with two hands, and his arm be expanded, and the doctor's hands be upon the neck and under him, and he press the joint with the knuckles of his fingers or with the palm of his hand rolled in so that it turns into its place.
But if the dislocation is to the front parts, then it returns so that he bends his hand with his hand, as he pierces the neck with the palm of his hand rolled in.
And if the dislocation does not obey the reduction, then administer a strong and violent extension.
And it is that two ministers extend the arm, and hold that joint so that it is not burned by the extension.
And then let the arm be rolled all around as it is wrapped in a folded cloth over his hand, or bandage.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, let it be anointed with some oil so that it is helped by causes which help in the lubrication of the joint with ease; and then press the joint with strong pressure as it returns.
And then its reduction let it be that the plaster upon which it is placed be stiffened and dried with egg white, and let it be stretched with decent tightness, and his arm suspended from the neck, and let him lie for several days.
And then he should be unbound; and if the joint is formed in its place, then you unbind his ligament and leave it.
And if you see that the joint is not stronger, then you repeat the plaster and the ligament, and leave it for a few days until it is strengthened; then unbind it.
But if there is a heaviness that happens to him in the reduction of the joint, and its disposition is in movement, then administer moisture by bath and subtle friction, compression while he is moved away; or pause over the joint a poultice, if it is a mobile joint; then bind it, and leave it for the day and night. And then stop that, and put him in a bath; and when he is dry, then you rub the joint with temperate friction, and then repeat on it an ointment, if it is swollen with fluid, once, and the second, and the third day, Already corruption has come about the wound' s innermost part, while it is filled with pus.
And if you want, pause over that split a wet and warmed-up cow's udder with blood and strengthen it over there; and do that several times, because that is wet and returns to its disposition.
Chapter .XXVIII.
Of the cure for a dislocation of malasan, which is of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
Malasan of the hand sometimes is displaced, and the reduction of its dislocation is easier, and the contrary is of the other joints, but however there must be velocity in the reduction of it in the hour in which it is displaced, before that the site is pustularized or pustules happen to it.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the joint of the patient's hand on a table and him extend his hand, and the doctor places his hand or his palm on the prominence of the joint, and press it in while it returns to its location.
But however there must be considered if the palm is already turned towards the palm of the hand, then the patient places the back of his hand on a table against the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is previous to the back of the hand, then the position of the hand of that one intrinically on the table, so that the doctor's hand boxes over that prominence of the joint.
And therefore if it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then strengthen that with plaster yielding the pustule and leave it, and do not repeat it, because otherwise, he will not endure or be able to bear the reduction of it until days have passed over him.
But however the joint is bent according to its tortuosity; and do not notice to the patient any cause, except that the hand is softened and cannot strengthen anything; then know that the nerves are pinched or cut, and then there is no remedy in him unless it is strengthened with cautery near the place of it and make it strong there is no remedy.
Therefore when the malasan is returned, then pause over it plaster which we have mentioned, and let it be left and strengthened for a few days; then unbind it and let his hand be exercised.
But if its movement is stopped and there happens to it some heaviness, then soothe that with warm water and friction a few times, until it is moved away.
Chapter .XXIX.
Of the cure for dislocation of the thumb.
Sometimes the thumbs are displaced completely apart.
And when any of those fingers is displaced to the back of the palm or to its belly, then press and push the displacement of it with your fingers, until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend that over the part to which it is displaced, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend that and extend that until it is straightened in that its day.
And when it is morning, loosen that according to that narration, and do not stop unbinding it in the day and exercising with movement, and loosen that in the night; and do that for a few days until it is tight.
And do that same thing with that if it is displaced to the belly of the hand, and loosen that to that part and do with it its first operation, until it is healed.
And likewise do with that if it is displaced to the other parts.
Chapter .XXX.
Of the cure for dislocation of the shoulder joints.
When there happens to any of the shoulder joints or the neck a complete dislocation, and they remain only the shoulders out of their places, then in that there is no cure, because death pursues the patient.
And the sign of that is that the digestion of the patient is outside his will, and he cannot retain it; and often times some members of him are softened or two legs of him or two arms of him, or one of those; or one joint is displaced out of its location, because often times there is displacement and the removal of those to four parts.
Then that one is displaced to the back parts and is named luxation.
But its cure is that then you consider if the luxation happens from childhood, because in that there is no cure or of that there is no healing at all.
But in that one which comes from falling or striking against those, then the Ancients gave the manner of cure with prolix speech, of which the most returns with profit; but I and the few excuse the excess of me, because of my proximity to my intention exposing it, with which I have exposed that and spread out that.
And of those none is healed except that one which happens in the shoulder precisely with that which I return to it.
And let the patient be extended over his face on a flat surface, and let him be extended under him instrument wet so that he is not bent at the feet of him.
Then a straight staff be placed, fixed in the ground at the end of the stake around the head of him, and another staff in a hole the feet of him in the other extremity of the stake.
And one minister holds the staff, and it is not fixed in a hole; and another minister has the other according to that form.
Then wrap around the head of the patient and under the tits let them be stretched by a lion-wasp; and extend the end of the band to the staff which is over the head of him, and loosen that in him; then strengthen the other band over the buttocks of him the knuckles of him, and under his legs; then tighten all the bands and loosen those in the other staff which is over the feet of him.
Then the ministers each extend the band to the end of the staff, and let the two staffs and the bands of their locations in which they are fixed not be displaced, but however do not stop as I have said before.
And the doctor pauses his palms over the shoulder with strength until it returns, or let him pause over that a table, and then adjust over the table his feet until it returns.
But if it does not return by that form, then take a table of which the breadth or length is in a hole four arms, and a cavity in the wall of which I have said before is near the patient a place into which the end of the table. And then place the middle of the table on the spine, and the doctor places his feet on the other end, and stretch well so that the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you want, open a turn which is twisted by hand; and it is that you place on the ground behind the head of the patient at the height of two fists, the length of which is one arm, and behind his head at the height of two fists; and let the distance between each post be a palm, and now there is made in each post a hole through which the rope passes, and each is firmly fixed to the ground so that it does not move.
And there is placed a round post in which is the turn, and it is wound around the two holes of the two posts.
And at its end there is a hole through which the post passes, the length of which is a palm where it is wound; and on the other two posts there are similar ones.
Then stretch the ligaments that you have tightened on the patient's feet in the hole that is behind his head, and the ligaments that you have tightened on his thighs in the turn which is behind his feet.
Then place each turn and have an assistant twist the turn with their hand, and the doctor bends the spine as we have said.
Then, after the spine has returned and the place has been greased, you must apply plaster of Paris mixed with egg white, and then stop it; and then place over the plaster a plate of wood whose width is three digits or so, and the length of which is the quantity that takes up the area of the hunchback, and over the spine its thickness.
And then it should be bound with ligaments as we have said, and let the patient take subtle nourishment until he is healed.
And if any swelling remains in the area at the end of the healing process, then let potions that soften and smoothen be administered by application of the table for a long time, which we have recounted; and now lead is applied to that place.
And sometimes swelling occurs in the back of the spine, and it is cured by that dislocation; and it is that bone which one should be wary of so that there is no protrusion to him by this medicine, for it will certainly bring about his death.
Chapter 31.
Of the cure for a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the pelvis and the joint of the muscle do not move except by dislocation only, and it does not happen to those which happen to the other joints of slight removal and concavity.
And the joint of the pelvis is dislocated according to four manners, for it is dislocated at its parts within, or at its parts without, or at its parts before, or at its parts behind.
And most often it is dislocated at its parts within, and rarely at its parts before or behind.
And the sign of its dislocation at its parts within is that when you touch the leg of the patient with your hand, it is longer and the hip joint is more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend his foot due to the tongue-like protrusion, and there is the place where the tongue-like protrusion is pustular due to pustules, so that the head of the thigh has come here.
And the sign of that which happens to dislocation at its parts without is that his symptoms are contrary to those above.
And the sign of that which happens to dislocation at its parts before is that he spreads his leg according to his will, but still cannot bend it without pain where it hurts in the hip; and if he tries to walk and cannot go forwards on the tiptoes of his feet, his urine is retained and there is pustulation where he walks, and he has to walk on his heels.
And the sign of that which happens to dislocation at its parts behind is that he cannot spread his hip, or bend it in front of him where he walks; and his leg is shorter than the other, and there is softening of the hip joint, and the head of the thigh protrudes towards that place.
However, the reduction of this dislocation is that you should consider, then, if the dislocation is old and ancient, and it has been aged by having it reduced and remaining according to its condition, then there will be no cure in that; and afterwards there will be no protrusion.
However, in the one for whom the dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four kinds of dislocation, then you turn to that which he wants and extend it towards its parts within and without, and also to his right and left, for it will return quickly and does not need another cure.
However, if it does not return in this way, then you should prepare a strong servant and extend the leg of him from the lowest part, or with his hands on top of them over the hip joint; and another servant extends his hand over his anus that puts his fingers below his testicles; and then stretch with ligaments on the root of the thigh, and have another servant hold the end of the ligament.
And let his extension be from the part within towards the ankle, or from the part behind to the part of the chest.
And let all those extensions be in one moment while the patient is elevated by his body from the ground and hanging bent.
And this species of extension is common to all four.
And therefore if the dislocation returns as we have said, then it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to all that I will say about the proper cure.
In the proper reduction of it, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is at its parts within, let the patient be placed on his back; and then place the ligaments over the root of the thigh in that place between the head of the thigh and the place below the hip joint; and then stretch the ligament to the highest part of the ankle towards the highest part of the furcula.
And then let another strong servant grab his arms and press the large place of the hip joint and stretch to its parts without by violent extension, for it will return to its place.
And this species is the most gentle cure by which that member is reduced more than the other species.
However, if he is stubborn and does not obey this species of cure by all means, then let his two feet be bound together with strong ligaments, gently, over both heels and both hip joints.
And let the length of each of his parts be the quantity of four digits, and let the leg of him be extended but the other by the quantity of two digits.
And then let the patient be suspended over the head of the post, and let him be in the middle; and let his length from the ground be the quantity of two yards.
And then command a strong young man to press the head of the thigh, and let another young man be suspended by the patient, and the other young man press the thigh with strength, for the joint will return to its place with speed.
However, in the proper reduction, when it is dislocated to the part without, let him lie on his back according to how we have recommended regarding his hunchback; and let the ligament be tightened over the hip joint properly and over his feet.
And then two sticks are placed, one at the feet and the other at the head; and the sticks are adjusted to the middle of the hip on which dampened cloths are wrapped so that the patient is not harmed by the stick being between their thighs, so that the extension to the parts below is not drawn.
And then each assistant extends to his part, and the doctor has his hand open in the reduction according to the art.
And if the dislocation is to the front parts, then ensure that the leg is extended slightly and it will be according to this narrative on the hip; and the doctor places the palm of his right hand over the collar bone of the patient, and then with the other hand he grips it tightly, and with this grip he extends the leg out to the foot at the part of the dislocation.
And if the dislocation is to the back parts, then it is not necessary that the patient's leg be extended out, and he must be elevated above the ground; when he is lying on his side with his hip dislocated outwards according to what we have said about his positioning on two sides, and he is on his face and his limbs are extended according to what we have said, then there is no worry.
And it should be administered with pressure using a board over the location of the joint.
So when the reduction is complete according to what we have said — and the sign of its completion will not be hidden from you, and it is that you extend both legs of the patient, and when you see those signs and the patient stretches out his leg and expands it without any excuse, then you will know that the limb has returned to its proper place — and then you should bring the two thighs together and apply a poultice and bind with a bandage so that the hip is not harmed in either of its parts; and let the patient rest for three or four days.
Then loosen the poultice and bandage, and compare one leg with the other.
If you see those signs in full, then know that the dislocation is already closed and then let the patient walk.
And if you see any cause for softening, then return and plaster it, and extend it according to your first extension, and let him be in three days.
Then loosen that, and make him delay walking for a few days, until he is comforted.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of neck dislocation.
The neck is dislocated in three ways: it is dislocated outwards, inside, and to the bottom but not to the front.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to apply his collarbone with his thigh; then know that the neck is dislocated.
And the restoration of all of its ways is that you make the patient lie straight, so that he extends his leg if he can on it; adhere two fingers to his face, and place the sole of his foot between your feet; then make someone else extend his leg while the neck returns to its place.
And the sign of its reduction is that his collarbone adheres to his thigh with effortless lifting.
Then plaster it and make the collarbone adhere to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then loosen those.
And do not use the patient for walking for a few days, until he is healed and comforted.
But if its reduction is not possible for you, then administer strong extension with the limbs of which in the cure of the hip there is preceding narrative.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the cure for dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle is sometimes hurt by small removal, and sometimes it is dislocated according to completion, and its dislocation is done either inside or outside.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the ankle swollen proceeding to the part to which it is dislocated.
But its cure for removal is of slight reduction, and it is extended with ease with hands and is wrapped until it returns.
But in its cure when it is dislocated according to completion, you must make the patient lie straight, and have a strong and valiant minister from behind press him on his hip.
Then you with your right hand take the highest part of the foot from above, and with your left hand take the lowest part of the foot in the location of the ankle.
Then pull the foot to you with your right hand, and then twice with the left, and extend with the left; and press the sole of the foot with the right because of the collarbone there is work.
And do that two times according to what we have counted.
Then press the sole of the foot on the collarbone the third time, and you are at the ankle.
So if it returns in one or two according to this manner, and you see the foot soft, it is good; otherwise, repeat the operation on it until it does return.
But if it is prohibited for you to reduce it with what we have recommended, then let the patient lie on his back on earth, and stretch a wooden post in firm earth, and that it be between his thighs, and you have already wrapped a cloth around him so that he does not hurt the patient.
Then the minister of him has his thighs, and then another minister extends the foot with their hands or with the limb that lies over the collarbone; and each minister extends against the extension of his pair, and the post is extended between the thighs of the patient, so that his body is not drawn to the parts below because of its extension.
Then the doctor grabs the dislocation with his hands, and another minister has the healthy thigh at the parts below, because the dislocation returns with ease.
So when the dislocation returns and you are shown the health of its reduction, then plaster it and extend that with strong limbs, and tie the foot to the collarbone.
And you must be careful or watch out for the nerves which are behind on the ankle, so that the limb over it is not vehement and lead that strongly.
Then let him be in two or three days; and if the limb is softened, extend that.
Then loosen that on the third day or fourth, and prohibit the patient from walking for 40 days. But if he is forced to walk before that space, it is not certain that the dislocation is not dissolved and ruined, and then that he does not receive cure.
And if it happens to him wound blister, then you must minister in the healing of him that in which our narrative preceded, at another location of cure and of fomentation, until it is healed.
Chapter .xxxiiii.
Of the cure for dislocation of the fingers of the feet.
Let him who is dislocated be greased with a little oil; and that which is not difficult, rather than being easy.
But if the dislocation is in any bones of the chest, then let him make the patient lie on his back on the ground or a table, and be lying as if he were walking.
Then you stand on your feet, and place your hand over what protrudes from the joints, and then press that with your foot with strength until it returns, and see that it is greased and there is no protuberance in the location.
And then place under the chest of the patient a board which covers the whole chest, to which are two ends, and then tighten that with decent and firm strapping for three days; and then loosen that and let the patient rest from walking for several days, until he is healed and his healing is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the kinds of dislocations that are done with a blow or with a fracture or with any impaction.
When any of those causes happen to you and you are proficient in the cure of that and in the restoration of him, many times death ensues.
And therefore you should not presume about the cure of him, but let him who is wise in the art, with a long experience, be merciful and compassionate, mild, not hard and presumptuous; and that he use in the beginning medicines which yield apoplexy cauteries only so much, and let the patient trust in divine judgement, except to him by whom you expect salvation from ruin, in the alleviation of the illness, and in which some hope appears. And then try to make him the reduction at the beginning of the cause, before he receives an apoplexy cautery.
And then if the member returns according as we want, then administer a regimen which yields an apoplexy cautery and cures the blow with that which convenes to him of the dry ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and it happens in the separated bones, then you are proficient in their binding, and do in that which we have said in the simple illnesses of that from which they come.
And guard and avoid according to your ability that of the instrument in the ways of fear according as my preaching is preceded to you; for that is better for you to your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more salutary to your true body, those in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasim perfectly from which God be praised. Amen.
